<div id="review_2326364680" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2326364680" />
    <a title="Susan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5837456-susan"><img alt="Susan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1372225128p2/5837456.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2326364680?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan" href="/user/show/5837456-susan">Susan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19307556-a-handful-of-dust">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2326364680" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3579178397408112766">This is an important novel of Evelyn Waugh’s; marking a much more serious, darkly witty and sharply observant style, from his earlier, comic novels. It is widely suggested that this novel was largely the result of his first wife, Evelyn Gardner, or ‘She-Evelyn,’ leaving him for another man after a year of marriage. If so, Waugh certainly had his revenge , as he bitterly skewers his ex-wife, and her lover, in print.<br/><br/>Brenda and Tony Last have been married for five, or six, years, when we meet them.</span>
  <span id="freeText3579178397408112766" style="display:none">This is an important novel of Evelyn Waugh’s; marking a much more serious, darkly witty and sharply observant style, from his earlier, comic novels. It is widely suggested that this novel was largely the result of his first wife, Evelyn Gardner, or ‘She-Evelyn,’ leaving him for another man after a year of marriage. If so, Waugh certainly had his revenge , as he bitterly skewers his ex-wife, and her lover, in print.<br /><br />Brenda and Tony Last have been married for five, or six, years, when we meet them. Tony adores his ancestral house, Hetton, his young son, John Andrew and his wife. Brenda, it is soon apparent, is bored to tears. When John Beaver - a scrounging young man, who lounges around bars hoping to be brought a drink, has no job and little income, but is a useful ‘spare man,’ ready to drop everything for a free lunch, or dinner party - takes up a half meant invitation for the weekend, Tony is appalled at his arrival. Apologetically, he leaves Beaver to be baby-sat by Brenda and that, without doubt, is a mistake. Although Tony is blithely unaware of what is going on, pretty soon half of London is aghast at their affair. While Beaver rises in their esteem, Brenda takes a flat and begins to attend every party in London.<br /><br />This is very much a book of two halves and (a little like “Brideshead Revisited”) the first half is much better than the second. While the first half of Brideshead is so sublime it makes up for the second being not quite so wonderful, this novel does not manage to carry off the trick quite so well. The second half of this was taken from a short story Waugh wrote and, certainly, there is much biographical material in this novel – as well as a truly shocking moment (you’ll know it when you get to it). <br /><br />Waugh not only turns his vengeful, bitter words against his ex-wife, and her lover (John Heygate), but his satirises his own lack of knowledge about their affair. It is sharply satirical, cruel, vicious and unbearable in parts – the ‘shocking moment,’ takes away any sympathy for Brenda (not that she is particularly sympathetic anyway) and how John Heygate ever showed his face in public again, I have no idea. Still, in parts this is brilliant. I am immediately moved to read the latest biography of Waugh, “A Life Revisited,” and never get bored of this, most brilliant, author. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3579178397408112766" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1582237235" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1582237235" />
    <a title="John" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21938800-john"><img alt="John" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1373055112p2/21938800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1582237235?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John" href="/user/show/21938800-john">John</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141183961" href="/book/show/827814.A_Handful_of_Dust">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21938800-john?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21938800-john?shelf=evelyn-waugh">evelyn-waugh</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1582237235" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3982942802795297868">If you like black humour this is an enjoyable book. There are some genuinely some laugh out loud scenes especially with Tony and Brenda's young son John. Coupled with insights to the decay of the upper classes and the weird rules they lived by. At times you want to shake Tony Last out of his placidity and it is a relief when he develops a backbone when his gothic home of Hetton is threatened by Brenda. I would also say that Brenda is a woman who gives villainy a bad name. <br/><br/>The switch to South Ame</span>
  <span id="freeText3982942802795297868" style="display:none">If you like black humour this is an enjoyable book. There are some genuinely some laugh out loud scenes especially with Tony and Brenda's young son John. Coupled with insights to the decay of the upper classes and the weird rules they lived by. At times you want to shake Tony Last out of his placidity and it is a relief when he develops a backbone when his gothic home of Hetton is threatened by Brenda. I would also say that Brenda is a woman who gives villainy a bad name. <br /><br />The switch to South American jungles and the insane Mr Todd and the reading of Dickens is both comedic and tragic. I enjoyed A Handful of Dust and can understand why it is considered one of Waugh's best novels. It was also interesting to read the alternative ending Waugh wrote.</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_76561417" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/76561417" />
    <a title="Trevor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/175635-trevor"><img alt="Trevor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565151545p2/175635.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/76561417?book_show_action=true">Nov 03, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trevor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trevor" href="/user/show/175635-trevor">Trevor</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/175635-trevor?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer76561417" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1581791656094087470">I don’t know why I thought this was going to be a comedy, but I did think that when I started. The problem might have been the title, the clear allusion to Eliot’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/400412.The_Waste_Land_and_Other_Poems' title='The Waste Land and Other Poems by T.S. Eliot' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Waste Land and Other Poems</a> - you can only really be either ponderous or funny if you allude to <i> The Waste Land</i> and I just suspected that this would be funny. And then it starts with a character who is on the outskirts of polite society – not unlike the main character in Waugh’s first novel <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30929.Decline_And_Fall' title='Decline And Fall by Evelyn Waugh' rel='nofollow noopener'>Decline And Fall</a>, and well, it just made </span>
  <span id="freeText1581791656094087470" style="display:none">I don’t know why I thought this was going to be a comedy, but I did think that when I started. The problem might have been the title, the clear allusion to Eliot’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/400412.The_Waste_Land_and_Other_Poems" title="The Waste Land and Other Poems by T.S. Eliot" rel="nofollow noopener">The Waste Land and Other Poems</a> - you can only really be either ponderous or funny if you allude to <i> The Waste Land</i> and I just suspected that this would be funny. And then it starts with a character who is on the outskirts of polite society – not unlike the main character in Waugh’s first novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30929.Decline_And_Fall" title="Decline And Fall by Evelyn Waugh" rel="nofollow noopener">Decline And Fall</a>, and well, it just made sense that this was going to be a comedy. Then it just made sense that it was going to be a dark comedy – but by the end this novel proved to be more dark than comedy - much, much more dark. <br /><br />I don’t know an awful lot about Mr Waugh and his background, but my guess would be that he belonged (in some sense) to the British aristocracy (or rather the landed version of that – the type of people who not only have titles, but also manors). That is, I always assume he belonged to a class of people that in his life time had become an anachronism. Waugh’s great talent was the sad one of being able to write about how this class of incredibly privileged people suddenly (I guess between the wars, but definitely after World War Two) had either disappeared from the English landscape or found themselves, a little dusty, in among the other curiosities on display at various National Trust Houses.<br /><br />I’ve just seen that A handful of Dust wasn’t Waugh’s first choice for the title of this one, but I’m going to tell you my version of this quote from Eliot and what it refers to in the poem. That is, what I think you are expected to know from this reference. From memory (and I’m the first to admit my memory is somewhat shaky) of the thing is that Apollo was rather pleased with one of the sibyls and decided to offer her wish. If there is one think that you really ought to have learnt by now from literature about this whole wish granting thing is that it needs to be avoided like the plague, but never actually is. All the same, it wouldn’t ever be much of a story if she just told Apollo, ‘Look, take your three wishes and shove them’. The bit that generally surprises me is that sibyls are supposed to have the gift of prophesy, so you might have thought she would have seen this coming, so to speak. Nevertheless, she sees a pile of dust in the corner and says she would like to live for as many years as there are specks of dust in the pile. And this is the wish that is granted to her, quite literally until she realises that it might have also been a good idea to ask for eternal youth to go along with an awfully long life. When Apollo finally takes pity on her again she is so shrivelled up she is living in a jar. And when asked what she would now wish for she replies that she would like to be allowed to die. (In a world increasingly cursed with ever increasing life spans and a boom in dementia perhaps this really is a myth for our times) <br /><br />There are going to have to be some spoilers in this one, I’m afraid – so before I do those, you might want to know what I thought about this book and whether or not I would recommend it. The short answer is that I loved this book and I would recommend it whole-heartedly. I really like his writing and I really like what he writes about. I can identify with so many characters in his books on various levels and always find the themes of love and love lost and love not so much cast-aside as rather carelessly left too close to the edge of the table only to see fall and smash into a thousand sharp-edged pieces all a bit shocking in that ‘and that too is my life’ sense.<br /><br />Time to look away if you are thinking of reading this one.<br /><br />I think many people who have read this book might think the main female character (Brenda) is a bit stereotyped and far too harshly portrayed. I haven’t checked other reviews, but look, I wouldn’t be surprised if that was said repeatedly in them and I can see that might be a criticism. There are times in the novel when it does feel like stretching credulity to breaking point to believe that she didn’t expect her husband to fight back. And even if he is a wimp of the first order throughout much of the start of the book (and having personally been that wimp) I’m not as sure he is so completely unbelievable. I also don’t think she is as unbelievable as first impressions might imply. People tend to love to see themselves as the injured party in any interaction and just because she started an affair, abandoned her husband, tried to take away from him the only thing he truly loved (his manor house, no less) and had been effectively making a fool of him in front of just about everyone he knew (even if, for the most part, keeping all this behind his back) is no reason to believe that she would not feel like the ‘injured party’ when it came to the divorce.<br /><br />We are incredibly self-centred creatures, we humans. And I don’t want you to think that this is presented as the story of some poor guy whose bitch of a wife frigs off with some no-hoper and stuffs up the life of the man who truly loves her only to find them both losing everything in the process. Look, that is certainly a reading of this book, but it is a pretty unsatisfying reading on some levels. This reading requires everything to be Brenda’s fault – and that is pretty hard to sustain. There is no question that, as in life, divorce really needs two people to get the tango going. <br /><br />If your wife is finding life so tediously dull that she would need to fall in love with an idiot like John Beaver then, really, you have to say that as a husband you might not have been doing enough to meet her needs. <br /><br />No one comes out of this book smelling of roses – but selfishness is the major theme being explored here. The most horrible line in the book would probably be the one said when Brenda finds out her son is dead. You know, ‘oh, thank God’ is never going to be the most endearing of sentences uttered by a mother on such an occasion. We may not all be overly fond of apple pie, but motherhood, well, that’s another story… Waugh clearly has no intention of making this woman the world’s most loved female character. I mean, we are pretty well crossing Lady MacBeth with Medea in this scene. But isn’t it just fascinating that Waugh, in a throw-away line at the end of the book, has her married to the man she says this to? Nothing is simple in this book - nothing is black or white.<br /><br />The other interesting theme in this book, and one that isn’t ever answered, although it is central to the question implied by the title of the book – is when do you give up hope? And particularly given how this book ends for Tony that really does remain an open question.<br /><br />Look, this is a disturbing book, this is exactly the sort of book you might expect to be written by someone who is witnessing the death of their social class. This is also a comedy in the sense that the characters seem exaggerated in exactly the way we expect characters in comedies to be exaggerated – that is, exaggerated to make a point – but the points that are made are points worth making, and points well worth reading about.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1581791656094087470" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="elementListBrownSeparator"></div>



  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_33399775" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/33399775" />
    <a title="Jesse" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1432036-jesse"><img alt="Jesse" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544746042p2/1432036.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/33399775?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jesse" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jesse" href="/user/show/1432036-jesse">Jesse</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141183961" href="/book/show/827814.A_Handful_of_Dust">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432036-jesse?shelf=1930-s">1930-s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432036-jesse?shelf=brit-lit">brit-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432036-jesse?shelf=read-in-2016">read-in-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432036-jesse?shelf=literary-modernism">literary-modernism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432036-jesse?shelf=flappers-gin-and-jazz">flappers-gin-and-jazz</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432036-jesse?shelf=bright-young-things">bright-young-things</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer33399775" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17134645275518300120">Waugh’s novel makes me think of a curious little pen knife kept under plate glass display at an antique shop: a decorative little handle, perhaps delicately wrought in chrome, looking charmingly innocuous nestled among the moldering paste jewelry and assorted tchotchkes. But then, with the flick of a finger, the blade appears—unexpectedly sharp, dazzlingly shiny, potentially cruel. Careful now: Waugh might cut you.<br/><br/><i>A Handful of Dust</i> is perhaps similarly deceptive, especially when read today. Like</span>
  <span id="freeText17134645275518300120" style="display:none">Waugh’s novel makes me think of a curious little pen knife kept under plate glass display at an antique shop: a decorative little handle, perhaps delicately wrought in chrome, looking charmingly innocuous nestled among the moldering paste jewelry and assorted tchotchkes. But then, with the flick of a finger, the blade appears—unexpectedly sharp, dazzlingly shiny, potentially cruel. Careful now: Waugh might cut you.<br /><br /><i>A Handful of Dust</i> is perhaps similarly deceptive, especially when read today. Like an alluring little relic of another age, it satirizes the values and behaviors of an era that today feel quite distant and removed. And yet, despite their obvious foibles I actually liked both Brenda and Tony Last, <i>Downton Abbey</i>-esque minor aristocracy whose happy-seeming seven year marriage suddenly but somewhat understandably begins to spiral. Quietly and unobtrusively things for the Lasts run off the rails, even if neither partner seems particularly discontent with their current situation. Instead each drifts about in a cloud of their own preoccupations and unvoiced concerns, making them oblivious to the fact that there is, in fact, quite a bit amiss. It’s a chilling indictment of how quiet enervation can be just destructive as fiery confrontation. <br /><br />Which perhaps gives a misleading description of a novel which remains on the whole quite jaunty. Not exactly laugh-out-loud funny, the humor is instead incredibly dry—and perhaps a bit brittle—employing wordplay and double entendre (ie “Lady Cockpurse”) and acerbic caricature (the failed, faux-oriental vamp “Princess” Julie Abdul Akbar). But stealthily the glamor of London society rituals give way to an underlying despair—it’s not for nothing that the title and opening epigraph are plucked from Eliot’s “The Waste Land.”<br /><br />Invoking that great landmark of literary modernism is apt for other reasons as well: though not as obviously experimental as Eliot’s poem, I was consistently impressed by the novel’s elegantly economical style. The reliance on dialogue and verbal interchange to convey meaning is almost cinematic, also emphasized in the way scenes are cut into small little snippets that skip around like so many jump cuts. Given the obvious technical mastery on display, I should have had more confidence in authorial control when the last third of the novel juts out into unexpected territory; I admit that for a while I was worried the story had gone irremediably awry just like the Last’s marriage. I need not have worried though, as everything plays itself out with a brutal efficiency and shocking force.<br /><br />The Penguin Classics (UK edition) I own includes an alternate ending as an appendix, originally attached to an American serialized version of the novel for legal reasons. It’s certainly a much more conventional resolution, but even if it doesn’t contain the same jolt as the intended conclusion there’s still something deeply haunting about it. Perhaps, it seems to ask, reconciliation was indeed possible for the Lasts via roads not taken. But for how long? At the very last moment a turn is taken, and the entire cycle seems to snap back into place, poised to begin yet again. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17134645275518300120" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

wake. <br><br>and yet, from what i have come to understand in reading waugh, he never would have let me have what i craved, and i have to come to believe he is much like the other e.w., edith wharton, after all. the tarnished polished people they describe wallow, and they wallow deep. there is no rising above here. there is only a hard, empty entitlement, or failure, or death. his books always have shocking deaths in them -- i have actually gasped aloud. i felt waugh had a much better handle on his characters here: they are more believable to me than the caricatures of the previous novels, and breathe, if pathetically, or malignantly. <br><br>update:<br>that last quarter ending i have since found had also been published previously and separately as a short story called "the man who liked dickens". it was in fact a prequel rather than a sequel. it is intensely creepy: never before has the reference to the works of charles dickens left with me such dread. and yet, i do think the casual reader might feel a slight disconnect when they embark upon this as a section of the novel. they feel like two different works, i think.<br><br>(when the lovely mariel left a comment, i did a bit more research and found a link to a paris review interview with waugh. he mentions this novel and the separate story and their genesis there, so i thought i'd post it: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener <div id="review_656771130" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/656771130" />
    <a title="Helle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6909470-helle"><img alt="Helle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1430669590p2/6909470.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/656771130?book_show_action=true">Jul 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Helle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Helle" href="/user/show/6909470-helle">Helle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140182392" href="/book/show/827815.A_Handful_of_Dust">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6909470-helle?shelf=english">english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6909470-helle?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6909470-helle?shelf=guardian-1000-must-read">guardian-1000-must-read</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer656771130" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4974613325781916599">Talk about bleak satire and cynicism! I read – and loved – Brideshead Revisited years ago, and once again we’re among the English upper classes, whom Waugh mocks more or less constantly throughout the novel, which is especially apparent in some of the ludicrous but funny dialogues. <br/><br/>Some of the characters are ridiculous (Princess Jenny Akbar, Mr. Beaver, ‘Mumsy’) , some are indifferent/oblivious to people around them (Tony), some are utterly selfish (Brenda), and most of the characters exhibit a </span>
  <span id="freeText4974613325781916599" style="display:none">Talk about bleak satire and cynicism! I read – and loved – Brideshead Revisited years ago, and once again we’re among the English upper classes, whom Waugh mocks more or less constantly throughout the novel, which is especially apparent in some of the ludicrous but funny dialogues. <br /><br />Some of the characters are ridiculous (Princess Jenny Akbar, Mr. Beaver, ‘Mumsy’) , some are indifferent/oblivious to people around them (Tony), some are utterly selfish (Brenda), and most of the characters exhibit a combination of all of the above, which means the not-upper-class 21st century reader essentially doesn’t identify with any of them but observes them as if it were a play at the theatre as they, the lead characters at least, go about ignorantly, yet willfully, digging their own graves. <br /><br />This is probably deliberate on Waugh’s part since according to himself he wasn’t interested in developing characters but in the usage of language as seen in drama, speech and events. Maybe that’s why most of the characters seemed flat and stereotypical and why we’re not really asked to relate to them. <br /><br />Most of the novel takes place at Hetton, Tony’s Gothic country mansion, and in London, where it’s all one big party and who is doing what with whom etc. Towards the end, however, Tony goes on a journey to his own ‘Heart of Darkness’, and it’s a completely different scenario, one that I at first felt was tedious reading about but which was ultimately quite brilliantly done and proved the final nail in the coffin of Tony and Brenda’s marital deroute. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4974613325781916599" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2376382569" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2376382569" />
    <a title="Roman Clodia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56111743-roman-clodia"><img alt="Roman Clodia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464021031p2/56111743.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2376382569?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roman Clodia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roman Clodia" href="/user/show/56111743-roman-clodia">Roman Clodia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140111507" href="/book/show/827817.A_Handful_Of_Dust">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2376382569" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10759221851069437853"><i>
  <blockquote>Although they were both in good health and of unexceptional figure, Tony and Brenda were on a diet. It gave an interest to their meals... 'Yes, darling, and when we get tired of it we might try an alphabetical diet, having things beginning with a different letter each day.'</blockquote>
</i><br/>At the heart of this book is the ill-matched marriage of Brenda and Tony Last ('she's lovely, he's rather a stick') - he obsessed with his family seat and the money it takes to keep up, forcing his wife to travel in the 3r</span>
  <span id="freeText10759221851069437853" style="display:none"><i>
  <blockquote>Although they were both in good health and of unexceptional figure, Tony and Brenda were on a diet. It gave an interest to their meals... 'Yes, darling, and when we get tired of it we might try an alphabetical diet, having things beginning with a different letter each day.'</blockquote>
</i><br />At the heart of this book is the ill-matched marriage of Brenda and Tony Last ('she's lovely, he's rather a stick') - he obsessed with his family seat and the money it takes to keep up, forcing his wife to travel in the 3rd class carriage of the London train; she frivolous, social, easily swayed by her fashionable friends. <br /><br />The first part of the book is classic Waugh: satirical, wickedly witty, derisive. Then, at almost exactly the mid-point, Waugh pulls off a stunt which left me breathless: a kick-in-the-guts moment which reveals something essential about the characters.<br /><br />The last third feels like we've wandered into another book altogether, a bit Graham Greene, a bit Somerset Maugham, even a bit <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/935885.Scoop" title="Scoop by Evelyn Waugh" rel="nofollow noopener">Scoop</a> as Tony turns into an explorer and goes off into the Amazon...<br /><br />So there's something a bit Frankenstein's monster about this overall, as if it's made up of limbs hewn from other bodies. There's certainly something of the Waugh of both <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1615370.Decline_and_Fall" title="Decline and Fall by Evelyn Waugh" rel="nofollow noopener">Decline and Fall</a> and the elegiac <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/903268.Brideshead_Revisited" title="Brideshead Revisited by Evelyn Waugh" rel="nofollow noopener">Brideshead Revisited</a>, but the final third, apart from the denouement, doesn't quite work for me - it's as if it has to be there to get to the endpoint Waugh wants, where the image of entrapment seems to parallel that of Brenda earlier at Hetton. <br /><br />Given that this is often seen as 'the divorce book' based on Waugh's own broken marriage, it's surprisingly not as savage as I expected, and Tony is ultimately given no more of a get-out clause than is Brenda. It's worth reading this as a 1930s update on the classic adultery novels like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19075.Madame_Bovary" title="Madame Bovary by Gustave Flaubert" rel="nofollow noopener">Madame Bovary</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/153.Anna_Karenina" title="Anna Karenina by Leo Tolstoy" rel="nofollow noopener">Anna Karenina</a> - with a distinctive Waughsian twist.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10759221851069437853" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2376382569">
  <td colspan="2">

  <span id="freeText15543303210403899945" style="display:none">I'm obviously in the minority with my rating. I've read a number of reviews here (after finishing the book) and am not convinced of its worth.<br />When I began reading this novel, I thought I might enjoy it. However, the inane characters and their inane actions soon dispelled that notion. <br />I get that it was supposed to be a satire - and a bitter one - but, for me at least, there was nothing there worth satirizing.<br /><br />On the back cover of the edition I read is the mention: "<i>A Handful of Dust</i> (was) selected by the Modern Library as one of the 100 best novels of the twentieth century." I wonder what those folks were on when they made that selection?<br /><br />Out of my house and off to the library sale with this one.<br /><br /></span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3100179094" />

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3100179094?book_show_action=true">Dec 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fonch" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fonch" href="/user/show/8647009-fonch">Fonch</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17219123796940410229">Ladies and gentlemen. In this case I take up a book, which I read late last year. I told you, I was very interested in writing some criticism of my Christmas readings, because, although I think, I didn't put any five. There was some interesting book and"A handful of dust".   <span id="freeText17219123796940410229" style="display:none">Ladies and gentlemen. In this case I take up a book, which I read late last year. I told you, I was very interested in writing some criticism of my Christmas readings, because, although I think, I didn't put any five. There was some interesting book and"A handful of dust". The first thing you've missed them is why did you put a (3) on it? The first thing, which I mean is one thing, that this three is, very misleading, and does not always reflect the reality of the whole. When I put a three books that I have liked, but that have been saved from burning for little, but also very good books, which aspire to a lot and for small things have not managed to reach my Olympus.<br />The first thing I must say is that the note in this book is not (3), but a (3.5-3,75). This is not a (3), for example as"A Tourist in Africa"that passed for very little <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12213176-a-tourist-in-africa-evelyn-waugh">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1...</a> <br />, or as"Loved Ones" <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7136270-los-seres-queridos">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7...</a>. Nor like"Scoop" this is not a novel, which he approved by the hairs, like the other two books quoted, but a novel, which reflects what is a three a good book, which I liked <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30919.Scoop">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3...</a>. No"A handful of dust" really happens to us as in Victor Hugo's wonderful novel"Los misérables" <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24280.Les_Mis_rables?ac=1&amp;from_search=true&amp;qid=XQjleziEMm&amp;rank=1">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2...</a> <br /> when the immortal writer of romanticism describes the Battle of Waterloo to us and tells us, that contrary to what we suppose or might suppose Napoleon was not in decline, but that we were in front of the best Napoleon, who was never better, and who only lost by god's will. Something similar can be said about Evelyn Waugh <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11315.Evelyn_Waugh">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a> <br />of this novel. Perhaps not the best Waugh, who, in spite of modern and secularist critics, who consider the best of Evelyn Waugh is his Pre-Brideshead period. I openly disagreed. I know Evelyn Waugh wouldn't be happy at all, that she thought her best novel was"Brideshead Revisited" <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30933.Brideshead_Revisited?ac=1&amp;from_search=true&amp;qid=dNvvGQhdRv&amp;rank=1">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3...</a> , , for me the best he wrote it from there. With all this book is one of the best Evelyn Waugh has written without hesitation, and a masterpiece, and it is much closer to the four, than to the two. I daresay this is one of the most moral and courageous novels written by the great English writer and the reason I didn't put the four stars on it was two. First because Waugh's great work was erected by the work of divine grace was coming and that was "Brideshead Revisited". Here providence is, and is glimpsed, but it is not quite 100%, as if it will be in later works of the great English Catholic writer (for me one of my favorite writers). Perhaps when you list your favorite Catholic writers, don't think of Waugh (even though he may have quite possibly written the best Catholic novel of the last century, with the exception of the "Lord of the Rings" <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33.The_Lord_of_the_Rings?ac=1&amp;from_search=true&amp;qid=bpX8s1LVgo&amp;rank=1">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3...</a> though I don't think, that the Waugh agree with me). All in all, I appreciate and love them, and I've been following Waugh's descendants with enormous. More out of devotion to the English writer than for anything else, because what interests me about them no longer has it, but I'm a faithful dog. This book is enormously moral, and despite it was written in the 1930s. It has been a prophetic book in every way. It is more than just a brilliant satire, such as "Decline and Fall" <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30929.Decline_and_Fall?ac=1&amp;from_search=true&amp;qid=R47EHNDxBY&amp;rank=1">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3...</a> or "Black mischief". This is one of the novels most hostile to divorce, ever written, and which shows the harm divorce causes in society. That's why we say, it's a very moral novel, because Evelyn Waugh doesn't put any kind of balm in and shows the destruction of marriage, like what is a catastrophe to society, and the tragedy that the protagonists suffer because of it. I put the Spanish case, because it is the one that touches me most present, but all those people who promoted the legalization of divorce, as then would promote that of abortion, and of people of the same sex, and now euthanasia. That society, perhaps, must have been very mindful of this book. The first Evelyn Waugh knows what he is talking about, because he lived it in his own flesh, when he divorced Evelyn, though I believe, that while he did not find the bliss because of the changes that occurred by the Second Vatican Council. I believe that his second wife the adorable Laura Herbert did something similar to what Domenica Plessington did for Guy Crouchback in the "Sword of Honour" trilogy <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30932.The_Sword_of_Honour_Trilogy">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3...</a> and it's getting things right for him. As far as possible it was a source of tranquility, for Evelyn, he endured the variableness of his temper, expoleoed his literary talent, was his greatest critic, and gave him the pleasures of marital life. That's why Waugh knows what he's talking about when he writes this magnificent novel that I didn't put four stars to for a reason, and it's because those characters. A friend of Evelyn Waugh perhaps that controversial friendship, which embodies the modern world, that Evelyn Waugh criticized, through Hooper. Cyril Conolly <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/51067.Cyril_Connolly">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a> he said that at first "A handful of dust" is the story of a happy family, and I add that it is destroyed by the superstition of divorce. What slain me about this novel is that the corrective inflicted by Waugh on his characters, though logical, and realistic was in my opinion a little drastic. The novel being extraordinary is magnificent, but also very cruel to the characters. Maybe they're cartoons, or screds, as brilliantly defined by Valle Inclán in his play "Lights of Bohemia" <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/65213.Luces_de_bohemia?ac=1&amp;from_search=true&amp;qid=JbfgfIeN7M&amp;rank=1">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6...</a> (perhaps the definition of sperm is of the unsalvageable in the overrated play of the Galician writer). They may be cartoons, and ridiculous characters, and I know Waugh will be mad from the grave with a server. But even though they're stupid, ridiculous, I came to love them except maybe the adulterous John Beaver, but the rest I came to love them with affection, and for that I was sorry for their luck. Because one of the things Evelyn Waugh shows us in this masterful novel is that there are fates worse than death, and this is the fate that inflicts on his characters. The author was realistic, and despite its comic background the work has a bitter character, but I, who am the enemy of divorce, saw me taking pity on those wretched creatures both Anthony Last and Brenda Last. Perhaps of all Evelyn Waugh's novels, they may have the bitterest fate. I know, I'm beating them up with this fictional character theme, but to me they're not fantasy creatures, but characters when they're created have a life of their own. My friends Jorge Sáez Criado, and Manuel Alfonseca <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14769537.Jorge_S_ez_Criado">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a> <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1117598.Manuel_Alfonseca">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a> have often been surprised to see what their fictional creatures were doing. In fact, my friend was surprised at what a character of his like Vicky Ramos was doing, one of his transition detectives. The character came to life, and did something the author hadn't thought of (it didn't come to such a dramatic situation as Kim Basinger's Cool film Gabriel Byrne, and Brad Pitt, nor an Unamunian ending as in stranger than fiction, or in Inkheart <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28194.Inkheart?ac=1&amp;from_search=true&amp;qid=CacVYjneMz&amp;rank=1">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2...</a> <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/25536.Miguel_de_Unamuno">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a> (That's why I don't like Writers like George R.R. Martin, who to please the bloodlust of their readers is dedicated to creating characters, and giving them the most disgusting deaths, that come to mind, because it is a way of wasting talent, and prevents them from developing. Death, or it must be a punishment or the culmination of a character who has reached his goal <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/43790-a-song-of-ice-and-fire">https://www.goodreads.com/series/4379...</a> <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/346732.George_R_R_Martin">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a> ). <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/183665-los-sabuesos-de-la-transici-n">https://www.goodreads.com/series/1836...</a> This subject my friend already treated my best friend in his very interesting "Jacob's ladder" <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24279520-jacob-s-ladder?ac=1&amp;from_search=true&amp;qid=PMgi7Hkknc&amp;rank=1">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2...</a> The writer must think of one thing, that he is a sub-creator, and that in turn he is a character of an even more wonderful novel, which ends up culminating in an end that of salvation. That is the destiny of every human being created by God. See if it's saved or not. That is his mystical quest and the ultimate end of all. That's the only one, but, or flaw I put to a masterful novel, that has it all. The so-called reference to myths in this case Waugh is based on the Arturic mythology and the relationship between Geneva and Lancelot to tell the adulterinous romance between Beaver (I've never seen the most evil beaver except that of Mel Gibson's film). We see that drinking from the classics was very common at the time, and Waugh does so with incredible, as he did in the "Sword of Honor" trilogy cited above, when he compares him to Menelaus, and his adulterous wife Virginia Troy to Helen of Troy. It's funny, but it's the opposite of what you do now. Fiction was previously adapted to classical canons, and now the myth is reinterpreted in a postmodern and postmodern, and relativistic way, to please exclusive lobbyists. It is a delight, and a great joy to see the almost-nobiliar atmosphere (something I always wish Waugh, who was a bourgeois of aristocratic tendencies, and in fact made us love the British nobility by overcoming the previous models of Maurice Baring's novels <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/13240.Maurice_Baring">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a> and Robert Robert Benson <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/330845.Robert_Hugh_Benson">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a> ) and bucolic in which both Anthony and Brenda move. For example, the parish priest's sermons are hilarious, he always says an ancient sermon, and he does not change it, even if it is from the time of Queen Victoria. In fact, the part of the tragedy is Brenda's tedium, routine, and monotony. That they have more to do with tragedy than the pathetic love attempts of John Beaver and his mother. Also Brenda a woman already made has something of Cougar, or MILF. Brenda's son and Anthony Last, though funny to me, i found him charming, especially the hilarious scenes he has with a suitor that Brenda is going to find her husband, to forget about her, and consent to allow her adultery or give him a divorce. I'll always have a doubt like Jenny Abdul Akbar's on-go-by-hued muley. Precisely this little character, who will star in his moment "A gone with the wind" (I can not reveal why, but if you have seen the film, or read the novel you will understand it). Anyway, there's almost deterministic Wugh. However, it is from the disappearance of this character, that while Christopher Nolan's wonderful film Interestellar explains that Murphy's law is not that something bad is going to happen, but that in the wake of a fact a chain of facts will occur. Interestingly it is from this moment on, when a succession of facts ends up destroying the Last, and in some cases giving them a fate worse than death. The characters' friends don't help either. . Although Anthony Last is a friend of his wife's former suitor. This relationship reminds me of the one Guy Crouchback is going to have with another ex of his wife. In this novel we will see one of the scenarios that will be important in "Brideshead revisited" the Old Hundreth, as Anthony and his friend will leave brown peaks just like Lord Sebastian Flyte and Charles Ryder. In fact, after the catastrophe, we'll see attempts by Anthony to fix his life, but with every decision he makes, he'll make it worse. Her relationship with a single mother she decides to date is curious. Social clubs are very well described, and the cold social system that will be Brenda's undoing, and that hasn't changed much either. . Because in the end divorce is almost never friendly (in fact, it's a failure of two people), it generates crunch, and it's an economic decline, for the one who gets it, and that's superbly described by Evelyn Waugh. Charles's case with his ex-wife Celia is very rare. One wonders if before taking such a disastrous step, but it is better worth attending a COF, or as Reverend Playfield says in "The Quiet Man." "Not worth fighting for your partner's love" <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/407432.The_Quiet_Man_and_Other_Stories?ac=1&amp;from_search=true&amp;qid=noIkpiTqiV&amp;rank=3">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4...</a> Also although in a parodic way Waugh offers us a great adventure, which will end up being terrible, and I will not give him the adjective that he intended to give him, because the shrewd user would discover what fate has in store for Tony Last. But it's a terrible outcome. A living death. So terrifying, he has appeared in several horror anthologies. As for Brenda, she also gets a punishment. Her fate is very dark and it was a fate from which Virginia escaped by the hair. In fact many Wavian scholars have wanted to see in an allusion to a certain Brenda a reference to this character. To sum up a great novel, which will make you think and reflect, apart from laughing, but I warn you, if you are too sensitive do not read it, even if you did you would miss a masterpiece of literature of the last century. <br /></span>

<div id="review_1775722946" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1775722946" />
    <a title="Greg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39346323-greg"><img alt="Greg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513962691p2/39346323.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1775722946?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Greg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Greg" href="/user/show/39346323-greg">Greg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39346323-greg?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1775722946" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15271955379503580939">England is between two World Wars, and when tragedy strikes a family in the form of a death, no one seems very upset. Are the English simply numb from the loss of almost an entire generation of men due to WW1? Or are the upper class simply ripe for satire? I think it's a little of both until towards the end of the book a woman wants a divorce so her husband basically hires a woman to spend a weekend with him so that he can be blamed for infidelity while the wife is the one who had been having an</span>
  <span id="freeText15271955379503580939" style="display:none">England is between two World Wars, and when tragedy strikes a family in the form of a death, no one seems very upset. Are the English simply numb from the loss of almost an entire generation of men due to WW1? Or are the upper class simply ripe for satire? I think it's a little of both until towards the end of the book a woman wants a divorce so her husband basically hires a woman to spend a weekend with him so that he can be blamed for infidelity while the wife is the one who had been having an affair. This is satire, surely this isn't a custom of any class of people. But then the story takes a turn for the weird, as the husband disappears and... well that would be too much of a spoiler to reveal here. There is no one to like here except Mrs. Rattery, who plays 4-pack solitaire as her way of dealing with the chaos of the death, the chaos of everything. She's outside it all, sometimes literally above it all (she flies her own plane) and goes her own way. And nobody in the book likes her. But they like each other: everyone but Mrs. Rattery can smell a rat a mile away. A book jacket blurb tells us that this is "Waugh at his most scathing," but I'm not clear on this point: the English are indeed, at this point of time, numb. I'm not sure if the English are ripe for a "scathing." This is an odd work, a bit uneven in tone, but compulsively readable. It's a page-turner in every sense of the term. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15271955379503580939" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1679548276" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1679548276" />
    <a title="Gary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45221326-gary"><img alt="Gary" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601699453p2/45221326.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1679548276?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gary" href="/user/show/45221326-gary">Gary</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140111507" href="/book/show/827817.A_Handful_Of_Dust">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Gary by: </span>
      Sas Tovee-Lynch
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45221326-gary?shelf=adventure-derring-do">adventure-derring-do</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45221326-gary?shelf=relationships-romance">relationships-romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45221326-gary?shelf=social-commentary">social-commentary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1679548276" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11015314331254729654">Overall I really enjoyed this book, though it changed so dramatically about half way through that I wasn’t sure at first if I liked it after that, because it was not what I was expecting.<br/><br/>The story is essentially one of upper class relationships and behaviour between the wars. The characters are filthy rich by most people’s standards but several of them never have enough money because they are maintaining enormous ancestral homes that cost a fortune to run. This is the cause of a lot of their pro</span>
  <span id="freeText11015314331254729654" style="display:none">Overall I really enjoyed this book, though it changed so dramatically about half way through that I wasn’t sure at first if I liked it after that, because it was not what I was expecting.<br /><br />The story is essentially one of upper class relationships and behaviour between the wars. The characters are filthy rich by most people’s standards but several of them never have enough money because they are maintaining enormous ancestral homes that cost a fortune to run. This is the cause of a lot of their problems: the ‘Lord of the Manor’ feels an ancient and heavy burden of responsibility towards his estate, tenants and most of the village, the populace of which still relies heavily on the estate for its economic wellbeing.<br /><br />This was a time in England when the value of agricultural land had declined and continued to do so and huge inherited estates became more of a burden than an asset. Many large country houses were being sold off to nouveau-riche Americans, who themselves were indulging themselves in their wealth – wealth born of a very different kind of business; a hard-nosed ‘life is tough, so fight for what you want’ sort of wealth, which many Americans then invested – squandered in some cases, no doubt – buying British country houses and turning them into hotels, schools and golf resorts. The people selling them these piles were often rent by conflicting emotions: enormous guilt and feelings of failure at having to sell up the family seat, sometimes centuries in the making: guilt towards their servants and tenants, whom they felt they had let down, guilt towards the older members of their families who did not understand that the world had changed and a system originally based on feudalism was no longer necessary or desirable. Mind you, they must have also felt huge relief once the money was in the bank.<br /><br />This is also a time when many rich people lived for their social lives (not having to trouble themselves with a job), when debutantes still ‘came out’ at balls (a system whereby young women were ‘introduced into society’, i.e. declared their availability for marriage), when lunching at the club or dining with ones friends was the highlight of their day. I guess that’s what some rich people always do, now and back then, though today it is all less formal.<br /><br />This was the late 1920s, a time when Victorian England and the Empire were still held in high esteem by many and, perhaps, mourned as the Empire crumbled and was given away (not before time) and the world had moved on enormously – the Great War had seen to that, but younger people demanded and got more freedom than their parents (as ever) and wanted more to life than duty and responsibility.<br /><br />It was also a time when the Victorian ‘system’ of marriage still operated; i.e. it was okay to have extra marital affairs if that meant you were kinder and remained loyal to your spouse ‘til death us do part’ and divorce was still frowned upon.<br /><br />Some spoilers…<br />A Handful of Dust is set in this atmosphere. It concerns a husband who feels the weight of his responsibilities as the ‘Lord of the Manor’, which he rather likes, and who loves his large but not very attractive or particularly historical house. His wife, on the other hand, hates living in the middle of nowhere in a not very attractive old house with not much to do, whilst her friends live and continually socialise in London. She is bored and a bit envious which, as we all know, often leads to trouble.<br /><br />She embarks on an affair with a young man who is quite good looking but pretty useless, having no money, no status and is a bit of a joke to Society. The story develops along these lines until the husband finds out and things come to a head – and then it all changes completely! I won’t reveal how but it seemed a huge leap in plot development.<br />Waugh was clever because truth is stranger than fiction generally and people do strange things when they experience a big and unwelcome change in their lives. Suffice to say that the main protagonists both get more than they expected and both look rather foolish.<br /><br />The book is very well written; Waugh is able to give sufficient characterisation in just a few words and the story certainly grips you. The ending is totally unpredictable until over half way through.<br /><br />I will read this one again and read some more Waugh.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11015314331254729654" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_57049267" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/57049267" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/426277-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584954318p2/426277.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/57049267?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/426277-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=humor">humor</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer57049267" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13894042183986417368">Evelyn Waugh’s <i>A Handful of Dust</i>, on several end-of-century Top 100 lists,was published on September 3, 1934. Waugh took the title for his novel from a line in T. S. Eliot’s <i>The Waste Land</i> — “I will show you fear in a handful of dust.” In <i>Brideshead Revisited</i>, Waugh returned to the same poem, sending Anthony Blanche out on an Oxford balcony to stutter a few lines from it. Waugh’s biographers have noted a particular connection to Eliot. Early in life, Waugh liked to associate himself with Eliot’s</span>
  <span id="freeText13894042183986417368" style="display:none">Evelyn Waugh’s <i>A Handful of Dust</i>, on several end-of-century Top 100 lists,was published on September 3, 1934. Waugh took the title for his novel from a line in T. S. Eliot’s <i>The Waste Land</i> — “I will show you fear in a handful of dust.” In <i>Brideshead Revisited</i>, Waugh returned to the same poem, sending Anthony Blanche out on an Oxford balcony to stutter a few lines from it. Waugh’s biographers have noted a particular connection to Eliot. Early in life, Waugh liked to associate himself with Eliot’s avant-garde style; in his late twenties, Waugh became a Catholic, as Eliot in his late twenties became Anglican; and later in life, both authors grew more conservative and wrote in support of preserving and improving the crumbling class system in Great Britain. <br />IN this novel we have a comedy that contains tragic events, but still manages to entertain the reader with Waugh's brilliant satire and wit. The protagonist, Tony Last, is an ossified country squire. As one of that system’s most doomed representatives when we first meet him, Last is living in blinkered bliss at Hetton Abbey, a rambling Victorian mansion renovated in tasteless neo-Gothic style. He is blithely unaware of his wife's peccadilloes. When the battle over divorce heats up Tony goes on an expedition to South America with a con man. Whether the trip is made because he is merely fooled by the con man or as a reaction to the divorce proceedings it does not work out quite as he expects. Eventually he falls under the spell of a madman named Todd who has a beloved set of Dickens novels; it is his passion to hear them read aloud, and it is Tony's personal hell to be the one required to do this.<br />This is Waugh at his satirical best and I can forgive his use of Dickens as torture (which reading him may be to some people anyway). While I had trouble understanding the foibles of most of the characters I understood enough of the story to become mesmerized by his brilliant satire and witty prose.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13894042183986417368" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('TR+3tOsVcy2bTPh585VVAtGsruBjXP0xoQlXcZFH4DytMm3X6HEP/MTOFkswANJDlm51H987dGYO/f+CPpZkAg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('fF0MKqqTr9RGz0joaIEGeCCL3eiJ6IbC2sDNy8Ki7gSccNZJqffTBRlNptqrFIE5Z0kGFzWPD5V1NGU4bXNqOg==')})
  }

Already oppressed by the appalling climate, frustrated in a loveless marriage, and belittled by the wives of more privileged officers, Louise wants out.\n \nFeeling responsible for her unhappiness, Henry decides against his better judgment to accept a loan from a black marketeer to secure Louise’s passage. This is the story of Jim Dixon, a hapless lecturer in medieval history at a provincial university who knows better than most that “there was no end to the ways in which nice things are nicer than nasty ones.” Kingsley Amis’s scabrous debut leads the reader through a gallery of emphatically English bores, cranks, frauds, and neurotics with whom Dixon must contend in one way or another in order to hold on to his cushy academic perch and win the girl of his fancy.\n\nMore than just a merciless satire of cloistered college life and stuffy postwar manners, Lucky Jim is an attack on the forces of boredom, whatever form they may take, and a work of art that at once distills and extends an entire tradition of English comic writing, from Fielding and Dickens through Wodehouse and Waugh. Today the knowledge they safeguard faces purposeful destruction and willful neglect; deprived of funding, libraries are fighting for their very existence. Burning the Books recounts the history that brought us to this point.\n\nRichard Ovenden describes the deliberate destruction of knowledge held in libraries and archives from ancient Alexandria to contemporary Sarajevo, from smashed Assyrian tablets in Iraq to the destroyed immigration documents of the United Kingdom’s Windrush generation. He examines both the motivations for these acts—political, religious, and cultural—and the broader themes that shape this history. He also looks at attempts to prevent and mitigate attacks on knowledge, exploring the efforts of librarians and archivists to preserve information, often risking their own lives in the process.\n\nMore than simply repositories for knowledge, libraries and archives inspire and inform citizens. In preserving notions of statehood recorded in such historical documents as the Declaration of Independence, libraries support the state itself. By preserving records of citizenship and records of the rights of citizens as enshrined in legal documents such as the Magna Carta and the decisions of the United States Supreme Court, they support the rule of law. In Burning the Books, Ovenden takes a polemical stance on the social and political importance of the conservation and protection of knowledge, challenging governments in particular, but also society as a whole, to improve public policy and funding for these essential institutions.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"800143259417712933\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return Howards End is considered by some to be Forster\'s masterpiece.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"7070030971232099715\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/11315.Evelyn_Waugh">About Evelyn Waugh</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/11315.Evelyn_Waugh"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1357463949p3/11315.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/11315.Evelyn_Waugh">Evelyn Waugh
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,780
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3240956018457796922">Evelyn Waugh's father Arthur was a noted editor and publisher. His only sibling Alec also became a writer of note. In fact, his book “The Loom of Youth” (1917) a novel about his old boarding school Sherborne caused Evelyn to be expelled from there and placed at Lancing College. He said of his time there, “…the whole of English education when I was brought up was to produce prose writers; it was al</span>
  <span id="freeText3240956018457796922" style="display:none">Evelyn Waugh's father Arthur was a noted editor and publisher. His only sibling Alec also became a writer of note. In fact, his book “The Loom of Youth” (1917) a novel about his old boarding school Sherborne caused Evelyn to be expelled from there and placed at Lancing College. He said of his time there, “…the whole of English education when I was brought up was to produce prose writers; it was all we were taught, really.” He went on to Hertford College, Oxford, where he read History. When asked if he took up any sports there he quipped, “I drank for Hertford.” <br /><br />In 1924 Waugh left Oxford without taking his degree. After inglorious stints as a school teacher (he was dismissed for trying to seduce a school matron and/or inebriation), an apprentice cabinet maker and journalist, he wrote and had published his first novel, “Decline and Fall” in 1928. <br /><br />In 1928 he married Evelyn Gardiner. She proved unfaithful, and the marriage ended in divorce in 1930. Waugh would derive parts of “A Handful of Dust” from this unhappy time. His second marriage to Audrey Herbert lasted the rest of his life and begat seven children. It was during this time that he converted to Catholicism. <br /><br />During the thirties Waugh produced one gem after another. From this decade come: “Vile Bodies” (1930), “Black Mischief” (1932), the incomparable “A Handful of Dust” (1934) and “Scoop” (1938). After the Second World War he published what is for many his masterpiece, “Brideshead Revisited,” in which his Catholicism took centre stage. “The Loved One” a scathing satire of the American death industry followed in 1947. After publishing his “Sword of Honour Trilogy” about his experiences in World War II - “Men at Arms” (1952), “Officers and Gentlemen” (1955), “Unconditional Surrender" (1961) - his career was seen to be on the wane. In fact, “Basil Seal Rides Again” (1963) - his last published novel - received little critical or commercial attention. <br /><br />Evelyn Waugh, considered by many to be the greatest satirical novelist of his day, died on 10 April 1966 at the age of 62.<br /><br />See <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evelyn_Waugh">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evelyn_W...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="3240956018457796922" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
